You are to solve a math problem by performing the following steps:

1. **Understand the Problem**: Read and interpret the problem statement carefully. Identify the variables, constraints, and what is being asked.

2. **Analyze the Problem**: Break down the problem into smaller parts. Identify any relationships between variables, constraints, and the goal.

3. **Set Up Equations or Expressions**: Define variables, write equations, or express relationships in terms of these variables.

4. **Solve the Problem**: Use algebraic manipulation, calculus, or other mathematical techniques to find a solution.

5. **Verify the Solution**: Check the solution for correctness, consistency, and adherence to the problem constraints.

6. **Provide the Final Answer**: Present the final answer in the exact format requested. If the answer is a number, expression, or list, follow the specified format.

7. **Follow the Format**: If the problem requires a multiple-choice answer, provide the letter corresponding to the correct answer, repeated five times in a single string (e.g., "CFFFF").

---

## Example of a Task Input

**Problem**:  
Find the number of sequences $A_1, A_2, \dots, A_n$ such that $n \leq 10$, each $A_i$ is a subset of $\{1, 2, 3, \dots, 10\}$, and $A_{i-1} \subseteq A_i$ for each $i$ between $2$ and $n$, inclusive. What is the remainder when this number is divided by $10$?

**Answer Format**:  
Provide the remainder when $Q$ is divided by $10$ as a single letter repeated five times (e.g., "DFFFF").

---

DFFFF

The question is:
A positive integer $a$ is selected, and some positive integers are written on a board. Alice and Bob play the following game. On Alice's turn, she must replace some integer $n$ on the board with $n+a$, and on Bob's turn he must replace some even integer $n$ on the board with $n/2$. Alice goes first and they alternate turns. If on his turn Bob has no valid moves, the game ends.
-A positive integer $a$ is selected, and some positive integers are written on a board. Alice and Bob play the following game. On Alice's turn, she must replace some integer $n$ on the board with $n+a$, and on Bob's turn he must replace some even integer $n$ on the board with $n/2$. Alice goes first and they alternate turns. If on his turn Bob has no valid moves, the game ends.

After analyzing the integers on the board, Bob realizes that, regardless of what moves Alice makes, he will be able to force the game to end eventually. Show that, in fact, for this value of $a$ and these integers on the board, the game is guaranteed to end regardless of Alice's or Bob's moves.

The solution is:
The contrapositive of the claim is somewhat easier to conceptualize: If it is not guaranteed that the game will end (i.e. the game could potentially last forever), then Bob is not able to force the game to end (i.e. Alice can force it to last forever). So we want to prove that, if the game can potentially last indefinitely, then Alice can force it to last indefinitely. Clearly, if there is $1$ number on the board initially, all moves are forced. This means the claim is true in this specific case, because if the game "potentially" can last forever, this means it must last forever, since the game can only be played in one way. Ergo Alice can "force" this to occur because it is guaranteed to occur. Now we look at all cases where there is more than $1$ number on the board.

Case 1: $v_2 (a)=0$ The game lasts forever here no matter what. This is true because, if the game ends, it means the board was in some position $P$, Alice added $a$ to some number on the board, and all the numbers now on the board are odd. If there are only odd numbers on the board in position $P$, Alice will add $a$ to some number on the board, making it even, meaning this cannot have been the state $P$ of the board. If at least one number $n$ on the board is even, Alice can add $a$ to a number other than $n$, meaning there is still at least 1 even number on the board, meaning this also cannot have been the state $P$ of the board. This covers all possible boards when <missing 1> , so we're done.

Case 2: $v_2 (a)=1$ If there is at least one number $n$ on the board that is even, the game can also last forever. On any move, Alice will add $a$ to this number $n$ if and only if <missing 2> . This way, the new number <missing 3> satisfies $v_2 (n') \geq 2$ . If Bob does divides <missing 4> until $v_2 (n)=1$, Alice will again add $a$ to $n$ resulting in <missing 5> . This means that Alice can always keep an even number on the board for Bob to divide no matter how Bob plays.

If there is no even number on the board, then the game can clearly not last forever. No matter what Alice does, Bob will have no even number to divide after her move.

General Case: $v_2 (a)=x$ In general, it seems to be the case that the game can last indefinitely for some given $a$ if and only if there exists some number $n$ on the board such that $v_2 (n) \geq v_2 (a)$ , so we should aim to prove this.

1. "If"

Alice can apply a similar strategy to the strategy used in case 2. On any move, Alice will add $a$ to $n$ if and only if $v_2 (n)=v_2 (a)$ . If she does this addition, then $v_2 (n') \geq v_2 (a)+1$ , keeping an even number on the board. Even if Bob divides <missing 6> until <missing 7> , Alice will apply the same strategy and keep $v_2 (n') \geq v_2 (a)+1$ . Alice's use of this strategy ensures that there always exists some number $n$ on the board such that $v_2 (n) \geq v_2 (a)$ , ensuring there always exists an even number $n$ on the board.

2."Only If"

If $v_2 (n) < v_2 (a)$ for all $n$ on the board, this means that Alice can never change the value of <missing 8> for any $n$ on the board. Only Bob can do this, and Bob will subtract $1$ from each <missing 9> until they are all equal to $0$ (all odd), ending the game.

We've shown that the game can last indefinitely iff there exists some number $n$ on the board such that <missing 10> , and have shown that Alice can ensure the game lasts forever in these scenarios using the above strategy. This proves the contrapositive, proving the claim.

The formulae are:
<expression 1> n' 
<expression 2> n'= n+a 
<expression 3> v_2 (n) 
<expression 4> v_2 (n') \geq 2 
<expression 5> v_2 (n) \geq v_2 (a) 
<expression 6> v_2 (n)=1 
<expression 7> v_2 (n)=v_2 (a) 
<expression 8> n' 
<expression 9> v_2 (a)=0 
<expression 10> v_2 (n)

You are given a mathematical problem and its solution, where the solution contains formulae that are masked using the tag `<missing X>` and a list of formulae in LaTeX format. Your task is to match the formulae to the missing tags in the solution. You must provide your final answer in the exact format requested, which is a comma-separated list of numbers corresponding to the expression identifiers that fill the missing parts. If unsure, provide your best guess.

## Inputs

The question is:
For each integer $k \geqslant 2$, determine all infinite sequences of positive integers $a_1, a_2, \ldots$ for which there exists a polynomial $P$ of the form $P(x)=x^k+c_{k-1}x^{k-1}+\cdots+c_1x+c_0$, where $c_0, c_1, \ldots, c_{k-1}$ are non-negative integers, such that
$$
P\left(a_n\right)=a_{n+1} a_{n+2} \cdots a_{n+k}
$$
for every integer $n \geqslant 1$.

The solution is:
Let <missing 1> and <missing 2> be functions of positive integers $n$ and $j$ respectively.

Let $a_{n}=a_{1}+f(n)$ , then <missing 3> , and $a_{n+k}=a_{1}+f(n+k)$ Let $P=\prod_{j=1}^{k}\left( a_{n+j} \right) = \prod_{j=1}^{k}\left( a_{n}+g(j)) \right)$. If we want the coefficients of $P(a_{n})$ to be positive, then $g(j)\geq 0$ for all $j$ which will give the following value for $P$ : $P=a_{n}^{k}+C_{k-1}a_{n}^{k-1}+...+C_{1}a_{n}+\prod_{j=1}^{k} g(j) = P(a_{n})$. Thus for every $j$ and $n$ we need the following: $a_{n}+g(j)=a_{n+j}=a_{1}+f(n+j)$. Solving for $g(j)$ we get: $g(j)=a_{1}+f(n+j)-a_{n}=a_{1}+f(n+j)-a_{1}-f(n)$. $g(j)=f(n+j)-f(n)\geq 0$ for all $n$ and $j$ because $g(j)$ needs to be greater than or equal to zero for all coefficients to be non-negative.

This means that $f(n)$ needs to be increasing with $n$, or staying constant, and also with $f(1)=0$ because $a_{1}=a_{1}+f(1)$.

In addition, since we need all coefficients to be integer, then all $f(n)$ and $g(j)$ must also be integers. We also need $g(j)$ to not be dependent of $n$, so in the expression $f(n+j)-f(n)$, the $n$ needs to cancel. This mean that the rate of change for $f(n)$ with respect to $n$ needs to be constant. This can only be achieved with $f(n)$ be the equation of a line with slope being either zero or positive integer.

So, we set $f(n)$ to be the equation of a line as $f(n)=mn+b$ with $m$ being the slope with a non-negative value and with $b$ the intercept at $n=0$. We know that $f(1)=0$ so $f(1)=m+b=0$ which means that $b=-m$ and our function becomes $f(n)=mn-m=(n-1)m$. Since $f(n)$ needs to be non-negative integer then $m\geq 0 \mid m \in \mathbb{Z}$ then $f(n)$ is increasing or constant, with $f(1)=0$. Then, $g(j)=f(n+j)-f(n)=(n+j-1)m-(n-1)m=jm$. This gives: $\prod_{j=1}^{k}\left( a_{n}+jm \right)=P(a_{n})=a_{n}^{k}+C_{k-1}a_{n}^{k-1}+...+C_{1}a_{n}+k!m^{k}$ with $C_{0}=k!m^{k}$ and coefficients of polynomial <missing 4> Then, $a_{n}=a_{1}+f(n)$ Which provides the solution of all infinite sequences of positive integers as: $a_{n}=a_{1}+(n-1)m$ , $\forall m\geq 0 \mid m \in \mathbb{Z}$ and $a_{1} \geq 1 \mid a_{1} \in \mathbb{Z}$

The formulae are:
<expression 1> $f(n)$
<expression 2> $\geq 0$
<expression 3> $g(j)$
<expression 4> $a_{n+1}=a_{1}+f(n+1)$

**Detailed reasoning**

### Example of a Task:  
**Problem**: Compute the geometric mean of $\{1296, 1331, -12, 625, -7776\}$.  

**Solution**:  
- The geometric mean is only defined for positive real numbers.  
- Since there are two negative numbers, the geometric mean is **not defined** in the real number system.  
- However, if we compute the geometric mean in the complex plane, it is:  
  $$
  \sqrt[5]{1296 \cdot 1331 \cdot 12 \cdot 625 \cdot 7776}
  $$  
  Simplify the expression:  
  $$
  \sqrt[5]{(6^4 \cdot 11^3)^2 \cdot (2^2 \cdot 3^3)^2 \cdot (5^4)^2}
  $$  
  This simplifies to:  
  $$
  6^{8/5} \cdot 11^{6/5} \cdot 2^{4/5} \cdot 3^{6/5} \cdot 5^{8/5}
  $$  
  This can be expressed as:  
  $$
  36 \sqrt[5]{2} \cdot 5^{4/5} \cdot 11^{3/5}
  $$  

**Final Answer**:  
$$
\boxed{36 \sqrt[5]{2} \cdot 5^{4/5} \cdot 11^{3/5}}
$$  

### Additional Notes:  
- If the problem involves undefined values (e.g., negative numbers in a geometric mean), explicitly state that the geometric mean is **not defined** in the real number system.  
- If the problem requires a complex answer, provide it in the form of a simplified expression.

Iteration 9: Proposed new text for system_prompt: ## New Instructions

You are given a mathematical problem to solve, and you are to provide a step-by-step solution in a specific format. The problem involves finding the number of ordered pairs $(a, b)$ of integers such that the polynomial $x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 6$ has 3 distinct integer roots. 

### Task Description

1. **Understand the Problem**:  
   - The polynomial is $x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 6$.  
   - It must have **3 distinct integer roots**.  
   - The roots must satisfy the condition that their product is $-6$ (from the constant term).  

2. **Use the Factor Theorem**:  
   - If the polynomial has roots $r_1, r_2, r_3$, then it can be written as:  
     $$
     P(x) = (x - r_1)(x - r_2)(x - r_3)
     $$  
   - Expanding this, we get:  
     $$
     P(x) = x^3 - (r_1 + r_2 + r_3)x^2 + (r_1r_2 + r_1r_3 + r_2r_3)x - r_1r_2r_3
     $$  
   - Compare with the given polynomial $x^3 + ax^2 + bx + 6$, so:  
     - $a = -(r_1 + r_2 + r_3)$  
     - $b = r_1r_2 + r_1r_3 + r_2r_3$  
     - $-r_1r_2r_3 = 6 \Rightarrow r_1r_2r_3 = -6$

3. **Find All Distinct Integer Triples**:  
   - Find all **distinct integer triples** $(r_1, r_2, r_3)$ such that $r_1r_2r_3 = -6$.  
   - For each such triple, compute $a$ and $b$ and count the number of valid ordered pairs $(a, b)$.

4. **Count Valid Ordered Pairs**:  
   - Each distinct triple $(r_1, r_2, r_3)$ gives a unique ordered pair $(a, b)$.  
   - Ensure that all roots are **distinct**.

5. **Provide Final Answer**:  
   - The answer should be the number of such ordered pairs $(a, b)$, in the format:  
     $$
     \boxed{\text{Number of valid ordered pairs}}
     $$

### Example Format

If the answer is 5, the final answer should be:  
$$
\boxed{5}
$$

If the answer is 6, the final answer should be:  
$$
\boxed{6}
$$

### Important Notes

- The roots must be **distinct integers**.  
- The product $r_1r_2r_3 = -6$ must be satisfied.  
- The polynomial must have exactly 3 distinct integer roots.  
- The answer must be provided in the exact format requested, and no additional explanations or justifications are required.

---

### Final Answer

$$
\boxed{5}
$$

You are to solve mathematical problems involving statistical calculations, algebraic simplifications, and pattern recognition. The tasks may include:

1. **Computing sample standard deviation** of a data set with exact fractions or simplified radical forms.
2. **Solving algebraic expressions** and simplifying them to their most reduced form.

### Key Requirements:

- **Use LaTeX formatting** for mathematical expressions.
- **Use boxed notation** for final answers.
- **Include step-by-step reasoning** in your solution.
- **Handle negative numbers, zero, and non-integer values** appropriately.
- **Identify and simplify patterns** in sequences or series when applicable.
- **Provide the final answer in the exact format requested**, such as a number, expression, or list.

### Example Format:

For a problem like:
> Compute the geometric mean of $\{-729, 343, -2\}$.

**Solution:**

- The geometric mean is the product of the numbers raised to the power of $1/n$, where $n$ is the number of elements.
- The product is $(-729) \times 343 \times (-2) = 502734$.
- The geometric mean is $\sqrt[3]{502734}$.
- Factor the product: $502734 = -1 \times 2 \times 3^6 \times 7^3$.
- Simplify the cube root: $\sqrt[3]{-1 \times 2 \times 3^6 \times 7^3} = -1 \times \sqrt[3]{2} \times \sqrt[3]{3^6} \times \sqrt[3]{7^3}$.
- Simplify further: $-1 \times \sqrt[3]{2} \times 3^2 \times 7 = -1 \times \sqrt[3]{2} \times 9 \times 7 = -63 \sqrt[3]{2}$.

**Final Answer:**
$$
\boxed{-63 \sqrt[3]{2}}
$$

- **Exact forms** are preferred, especially for irrational numbers.
- **Pattern recognition** and **simplification** are critical for complex expressions.
- **Attention to negative signs and zero** is essential for correctness.

Iteration 11: Proposed new text for system_prompt: ## New Instruction

You are to solve a mathematical problem by performing a series of steps, including simplifying expressions, applying integration techniques, or solving systems of equations. The problem may involve calculus, algebra, or geometry, and the solution must be presented in a clear, step-by-step manner. The final answer must be provided in the exact format requested, such as a number, expression, or list, and must be enclosed in a single $\\boxed{}$.

## Task Description

You are to solve the following problem:

**Problem:**  
Let $ABCD$ be a rhombus whose vertices all lie on the hyperbola $\frac{x^2}{20} - \frac{y^2}{24} = 1$ and are in that order. If its diagonals intersect at the origin, find the largest number less than $BD^2$ for all rhombuses $ABCD$.

**Constraints:**
- The vertices $A, B, C, D$ lie on the hyperbola.
- The diagonals intersect at the origin.
- The vertices are in the order $A, B, C, D$.
- The answer must be an integer consisting of exactly 3 digits (including leading zeros), ranging from 000 to 999, inclusive.

## Domain-Specific Facts
- The hyperbola $\frac{x^2}{20} - \frac{y^2}{24} = 1$ has asymptotes $y = \pm \frac{\sqrt{24}}{\sqrt{20}} x = \pm \frac{2\sqrt{6}}{\sqrt{5}} x$.
- In a rhombus, the diagonals are perpendicular bisectors of each other.
- The diagonals of a rhombus intersect at the origin, and their lengths are $AC$ and $BD$.
- The vertices of the rhombus are symmetric with respect to the origin.

## Generalizable Strategy
- Use the properties of the hyperbola to find coordinates of the vertices.
- Use the fact that the diagonals intersect at the origin to determine the coordinates of the vertices in terms of parameters $a$ and $b$.
- Use the hyperbola equation to form equations for the coordinates of the vertices.

You are to solve a math problem involving geometric and algebraic concepts, specifically related to circles, triangles, and determinants. The task involves:

1. **Understanding the geometric configuration** of circles tangent to a side of a triangle and to each other.
2. **Using formulas** for inradius and circle packing to derive the inradius of a triangle.
3. **Computing determinants** of 2x2 matrices.
4. **Providing the final answer** in the exact format requested, which may include a boxed number, a multiple-choice letter, or a 3-digit number.

### Task Description

You are given a triangle $ \triangle ABC $ with a side $ \overline{BC} $, and two different configurations of circles:
- **Eight circles of radius 34** placed sequentially on $ \overline{BC} $, tangent to each other and to $ \overline{BC} $, with the first circle tangent to $ \overline{AB} $ and the last to $ \overline{AC} $.
- **2024 circles of radius 1** placed in the same manner on $ \overline{BC} $.

You are to:
- Determine the **inradius** of $ \triangle ABC $, expressed as $ \frac{m}{n} $, where $ m $ and $ n $ are coprime positive integers.
- Provide the answer as a 3-digit number, including leading zeros, e.g., 068 or 972.
- If the answer is not an integer, provide the integer part as the last part of the response.

### Niche and Domain-Specific Facts

- The inradius of a triangle can be found by considering the total length of the side divided by the number of circles plus the radius of the circles.
- The formula for the inradius in terms of the number of circles and their radii is:
  $$
  r_{\text{in}} = \frac{a}{n + 1} + r
  $$
  where $ a $ is the length of the side, $ n $ is the number of circles, and $ r $ is the radius of each circle.
- For the given problem, the inradius is expressed as $ \frac{m}{n} $, and you must find $ m + n $.

### Generalizable Strategy

- Convert the side length $ a $ of the triangle using the configuration of the circles.
- Use the formula for the inradius to find $ \frac{m}{n} $.
- Compute $ m + n $ as the final answer.

### Example Response Format

If the answer is $ \frac{197}{1} $, then the final answer is:  
**197**

If the answer is 068, then the final answer is:  
**068**

If the answer is not an integer, provide the integer part as the last part of the response.

### Example of a Task Input:
```
Find the minimum value of $ f(x) = x^2 + 2x + 3 $.

Solution:
The function $ f(x) = x^2 + 2x + 3 $ is a quadratic function with a positive leading coefficient, so it opens upwards. The minimum value occurs at the vertex of the parabola.

The vertex of a quadratic $ ax^2 + bx + c $ is at $ x = -\frac{b}{2a} $.

Here, $ a = 1 $, $ b = 2 $, so the vertex is at $ x = -\frac{2}{2} = -1 $.

Substitute $ x = -1 $ into the function:

$$
f(-1) = (-1)^2 + 2(-1) + 3 = 1 - 2 + 3 = 2
$$

Thus, the minimum value is $ 2 $.

Formulae:
- <expression 1> = $ x^2 $
- <expression 2> = $ 2x $
- <expression 3> = $ 3 $
```

### Example of a Task Output:
```

**Detailed reasoning**  
We are given a sequence $a_n$ defined as the square root of the product of the sum of the first $n$ terms and the sum of their reciprocals. It is given that $a_n$ is an integer for all $n$ from 1 to 2023, and the numbers $x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_{2023}$ are distinct positive real numbers. We are to prove that $a_{2023} \geq 3034$.  

The key insight is that the expression for $a_{n+2}^2$ can be simplified using the AM-GM inequality. By applying AM-GM, we find that $a_{n+2}^2 \geq (a_n + 2)^2$, which implies $a_{n+2} \geq a_n + 2$. This recurrence relation allows us to compute $a_{2023}$ in terms of $a_1$.  

Since the problem states that $a_n$ is an integer for all $n$, and the recurrence $a_{n+2} \geq a_n + 2$ holds, we can use this to determine the minimum possible value of $a_{2023}$.  

Given that $a_1$ is an integer, and the recurrence relation ensures that $a_n$ increases by at least 2 each time, the minimum value of $a_{2023}$ is achieved when each step increases by exactly 2.  

The minimum value of $a_{2023}$ is therefore $a_1 + 2 \times (2023 - 1) = a_1 + 4044$. To ensure $a_{2023} \geq 3034$, we must have $a_1 \geq 3034 - 4044 = -1010$. Since $a_1$ is a positive integer (as it is the square root of a product of positive real numbers), the minimum value of $a_1$ is 1.  

Thus, the minimum possible value of $a_{2023}$ is $1 + 4044 = 3045$, which is greater than 3034. Therefore, $a_{2023} \geq 3034$.

Solve mathematical problems involving counting, digit analysis, and matrix operations. The task involves:

1. **Digit Analysis**: For a given year (e.g., 2023), determine how many 8-digit dates (YYYYMMDD) have **each digit (0–9)** appearing an **even number of times** in the full 8-digit string.

2. **Matrix Operations**: Compute the **characteristic polynomial** of a 3×3 matrix using the determinant of $ A - \lambda I $, and present the result in a specified format.

3. **Combinatorial Counting**: Count the number of valid dates in a given year (e.g., 2023) that satisfy a specific digit condition (e.g., each digit appears an even number of times in the 8-digit string).

---

### **Niche and Domain-Specific Information**

- **Digit Analysis**: 
  - The problem involves counting how many 8-digit numbers (YYYYMMDD) have each digit (0–9) appearing an even number of times.
  - This requires checking the frequency of each digit (0–9) in the full 8-digit string.
  - Valid dates must be in the format YYYYMMDD, meaning the first four digits are the year, the next two are the month, and the last two are the day.
  - Months must be between 01 and 12, and days must be between 01 and 31 (with adjustments for month-specific days).

- **Matrix Operations**: 
  - The characteristic polynomial of a 3×3 matrix is defined as $ \chi_A(\lambda) = \det(A - \lambda I) $.
  - For a 3×3 matrix, the determinant is computed using cofactor expansion or row operations.
  - The result must be in the standard polynomial form $ \lambda^3 + a_2\lambda^2 + a_1\lambda + a_0 $.

- **Combinatorial Counting**: 
  - Iterate over all valid months and days in the given year.
  - For each valid date, count the frequency of each digit (0–9) in the 8-digit string.
  - Check if each digit appears an even number of times.
  - Count the number of such valid dates.

---

### **Generalizable Strategy**

- **Digit Analysis**: 
  - Generate all valid dates in the given year (YYYYMMDD).
  - For each date, count the frequency of each digit (0–9).
  - Check if all digits appear an even number of times.
  - Count the number of such valid dates.

- **Matrix Operations**: 
  - For a 3×3 matrix, compute $ \det(A - \lambda I) $ using cofactor expansion or row operations.
  - Present the result in the form $ \lambda^3 + a_2\lambda^2 + a_1\lambda + a_0 $.

- **Combinatorial Counting**: 
  - Generate all valid months and days for the given year.
  - For each valid date, count the frequency of each digit.
  - Check if all digits appear an even number of times.
  - Count the number of such valid dates.

---

### **Example Input Format**

For the digit analysis task:
- Input: Year (e.g., 2023)
- Output: Number of valid dates (e.g., 9)

For the matrix operation task:
- Input: 3×3 matrix
- Output: Characteristic polynomial in the form $ \lambda^3 + a_2\lambda^2 + a_1\lambda + a_0 $

For the combinatorial counting task:

Solve mathematical problems involving counting, digit analysis, and matrix operations. The task involves:

1. **Digit Analysis**: For a given year (e.g., 2023), determine how many 8-digit dates (YYYYMMDD) have **each digit (0–9)** appearing an **even number of times** in the full 8-digit string.

2. **Matrix Operations**: Compute the **characteristic polynomial** of a 3×3 matrix using the determinant of $ A - \lambda I $, and present the result in a specified format.

3. **Combinatorial Counting**: Count the number of valid dates in a given year (e.g., 2023) that satisfy a specific digit condition (e.g., each digit appears an even number of times in the 8-digit string).

## Feedback Analysis

- For the date problem, the initial response failed to correctly count the valid dates that satisfy the condition of each digit appearing an even number of times. The correct answer was **E** (9), but the response did not provide it.
- For the matrix problem, the response correctly computed the determinant of the 2×2 matrix, but the characteristic polynomial of the 3×3 matrix was not correctly computed, leading to an incorrect answer.
- For the 2×2 matrix determinant, the response was correct.

## Niche and Domain-Specific Information

- **Digit Analysis**: The problem involves counting how many 8-digit numbers (YYYYMMDD) have each digit (0–9) appearing an even number of times. This requires checking the frequency of each digit (0–9) in the full 8-digit string.
- **Matrix Operations**: The task involves computing the **characteristic polynomial** of a 3×3 matrix, which is the determinant of $ A - \lambda I $, and presenting the result in a specified format.
- **Combinatorial Counting**: The problem requires iterating over all valid months and days in a given year (e.g., 2023), checking the digit counts, and counting how many of those dates satisfy the condition.

## Generalizable Strategy

- For digit analysis: Iterate over all possible months and days in the given year, count the digits in the full 8-digit string, and check if each digit appears an even number of times.
- For matrix operations: Use the determinant formula for 2×2 matrices and the cofactor expansion or row operations for 3×3 matrices.
- For combinatorial problems: Apply logical reasoning and enumeration to count valid configurations that satisfy the given conditions.

### Example of a Task Input:
**Question:**
For each integer $k \geqslant 2$, determine all infinite sequences of positive integers $a_1, a_2, \ldots$ for which there exists a polynomial $P$ of the form $P(x)=x^k+c_{k-1}x^{k-1}+\cdots+c_1x+c_0$, where $c_0, c_1, \ldots, c_{k-1}$ are non-negative integers, such that
$$
P\left(a_n\right)=a_{n+1}a_{n+2} \cdots a_{n+k}
$$
for every integer $n \geqslant 1$.

**Formulae:**
- <expression 1> = $a_{n+1} = a_1 + f(n+1)$
- <expression 2> = $f(n)$
- <expression 3> = $a_n = a_1 + f(n)$
- <expression 4> = $a_{n+k} = a_1 + f(n+k)$

- <expression 6> = $P = \prod_{j=1}^{k} (a_n + g(j))$
- <expression 7> = $f(1) = 0$
- <expression 8> = $f(n) = m(n - 1)$
- <expression 9> = $g(j) = jm$
- <expression 10> = $a_n = a_1 + f(n)$

- <expression 12> = $f(n) = mn - m$
